## 7,192

B"H, 8 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter from 3 Adar, the month in which we increase in joy (as well as your previous letters), in which you write regarding the affairs of the yeshiva.

I am confident that just as you have witnessed the wonders of Hashem in the past through the arousal of the *rachamei rabim* (great mercies) of our Rebbes, so too, and even more so, will it be in the future. This is especially true now that, with Hashem’s help, you are ascending in holiness from year to year, even more so than at the beginning, which was difficult, as mentioned above.

May it be Hashem’s will that everyone engages in this with true joy. The joy that the Torah commands is, in the words of our Alter Rebbe (Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chaim, 529:12), not to indulge in wine, etc., and to say, "Whoever adds to this will increase in the mitzvah of joy," etc. Rather, we are commanded only regarding the joy that involves the *avodah* of the Creator of all, etc.—see there.

And although the joy of Purim is distinct, being of the level of *ad d’lo yada* (until one does not know), unlike the joy of *Yom Tov* discussed in the Shulchan Aruch there, the common denominator between them is that this joy, too, must be an *avodah* of the Creator of all, only that it is without limitation, as explained in *Chassidus* in the *maamarim* of Purim.

It is my hope that you will utilize your potential as much as possible to draw forth the "wine of Torah," as explained in *Chassidus* in many places, which alludes to the secrets of the Torah—the *pnimiyus haTorah* (inner dimension of Torah)—which in our generation has been revealed through the teachings of Chassidus. This should be done in a manner of dissemination without limitation, as mentioned above. May it be His will that you do this with joy and a glad heart.

With blessings for good tidings and a happy Purim.

#### ***7,192***

**May it be His will:** A similar version was written to several others.

## 7593

B"H, 8 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn

To the esteemed and distinguished, etc., R' Ephraim, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter of 3 Adar.

Thank you for writing, albeit briefly, about the Shabbos of Toras HaChassidus in your camp. I wish to trust that the positive impression you describe will yield results in accordance with the will of our Rebbes, the Nesi'im—that farbrengens and gatherings of this nature, in addition to the inherent greatness of the matter itself, must bring about an increase in matters of Torah and mitzvos, in accordance with the teaching of the Mishnah: "The deed is the essential thing."

It is obvious that I do not mean a mere sermon, but rather that as a resident of the place, you should do what depends on you in the direction mentioned above. And through this, to influence others so that they, too, will strive to influence others—"one who moistens [others] in order to moisten [yet others]"—and we have been promised that one who comes to purify himself is assisted.

At a time of favor, mention all those you wrote about at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, and he is included, in accordance with the content of your letter.

Regarding your question as to whether to endeavor to bring your nephew, may he live, and his family to Eretz Yisrael, may it be rebuilt and established, it is understood that this is the right thing to do. However, first and foremost, one must clarify whether they agree to this, as the intention is to carry out the aforementioned in a legal manner, and one must obtain their consent. The methods of endeavor are known in your surroundings, for many have done as mentioned above and succeeded. It seems there are also offices that specialize in this, and they know in what style it is best to phrase the request; after all, there is no expert like the one with experience.

Regarding what you wrote about your dispute, it is understood that you must act according to the opinion of a lawyer who knows not only the written law, but also the reaction of the judges, for sometimes that is what is decisive.

May it be His will that they advise you for the true good, and may you bring good news. We are currently in the month of Adar, when we are commanded to increase in joy every single day.

With blessings for good tidings and a happy Purim,

**M. Schneerson**

## 7594

B"H, 10 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of February 22: when you include the mother’s name of each person you are writing about (as is the custom when mentioning them in prayer), I will mention them at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

Regarding the tone of your letter, I must offer a fundamental observation: the source of all blessings and success is the Creator of the world and its **Guide**. "Do not put your trust in princes, nor in a son of man, in whom there is no salvation." A person can only arouse mercy, exert effort in prayer, and at the same time point the way for the one in need of a blessing. In the language of our Sages, the prayer of the sick person or the one in need of a blessing is effective when channeled through our Torah—which comes from the word *hora'ah* (instruction)—the Torah of life, which instructs us how to live in order to receive the blessing of the Giver of the Torah, the Creator of the world and its Guide. It is understood, and indeed simple, that one must conduct their daily life in accordance with the instructions of our Torah, the Torah of life, and through the fulfillment of its *mitzvos*, regarding which it is said, "and he shall live by them." The more one increases in this, the more the blessing of Hashem, may He be blessed, increases in all that a person and their household requires.

If in past eras there was still room for debate and the demand for proofs regarding the above, there is no need for this (to our regret) in these years. Every single Jew walking the face of the earth—may Hashem lengthen the years of each and every one in goodness and pleasantness—is a "brand plucked from the fire," having survived the most terrible decrees of annihilation, for which, by the laws of nature, there should have been no place, Heaven forbid. This is especially true for those located in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established, as is known regarding the situation during the invasion of General Rommel, who stood in Alexandria, Egypt, with his entire army, and the rest is understood.

It is understood that my intention is not to preach, but rather to address the future and the conduct of daily life as mentioned above. We see tangibly that when one decides with the appropriate resolve to act in this way, the difficulties in reality are fewer and less significant than one had initially anticipated. This is provided that one is not deterred by the obstacles and difficulties that are common at the beginning of any endeavor or change in conduct, as the saying goes, "All beginnings are difficult."

And if all the above applies to every individual, it applies all the more so to the education of sons and daughters. As is well known, the youth are like a seed or a sapling; any slight improvement in them (and conversely, any slight deficiency or scratch) can have the most significant impact when they grow into a fruit-bearing tree, and the rest is understood.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above.

P.S.
In my secretariat, they do not write in Yiddish, which is why this letter is in Hebrew. However, you may write in Yiddish, as I am able to read that language.

## 7,795

B"H, 10 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

It has been a very long time since I have received a letter or news from you. May it be G-d’s will that this is a sign that everything is in order, both in general matters and in personal affairs.

I trust that he has conveyed my general regards, as well as what I discussed with him regarding your camp in particular and the countries in general. It is astonishing—and also distressing—that almost nothing is being done regarding the *hafatzas hamaayanos*. It is surely understood that, first and foremost, the responsibility lies with you and those like you, for you have merited to study for quite some time in the Yeshiva Tomchei Temimim, founded and directed by our Rebbeim, the *Nesi'im* (may the merit of the righteous protect us). The actions and words of the *tzaddikim* endure forever, and the "rock from which you were hewn" also assists you; surely, for one such as you, any elaboration on this matter is superfluous. It is obvious that my intention is not to preach, but rather to awaken and encourage you to engage in this dissemination and these activities with all the necessary vigor. My astonishment at this lack of activity is all the greater during this period, when the entire world—in the most diverse circles—has spoken about and disseminated at least points of the *maayanos* in connection with the bicentennial year of the *histalkus* and *hilula* of the Baal Shem Tov. In many places, they continue to do so even now [for example, here in the **coming** weeks, and this is being done by **non-religious** circles]. How much more so, then, does this apply to those for whom this matter is an internal, essential part of their being.

May I receive good news regarding all of the above. As the phrasing of the Megillas Purim goes [for the Megillah is read* starting from the following day], "The Jews had light"—this refers to Torah. The concept of "light" is to illuminate a place that was previously lacking. This is especially so according to the teachings of our Alter Rebbe, that "light" (*orah*) is an active verb (*lashon maf'il*); see *Shaarei Orah* on this (at the end of the book).

With respect and blessings for a *Purim samei'ach* and for good tidings.

#### ***7,795***

**Copy of letter:** See the following letter, and above, letter 7,785.

## 7,596

B"H, 11 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Rabbi, man of action, dedicated to Torah and mitzvos, a man of vigor, etc., Moreinu HaRav Chananya Yom Tov Lipa, may he live:

Peace and blessing!

I was pleased to see you in person, in addition to your letter from Sunday and the attachment included with it. May the Healer of the sick, the Healer of all flesh who performs wonders, bless you. May it be His will that your recovery be complete and swift, until you return to your full strength—both in the literal sense and in the deeper sense, as explained in the maamar *Maskil L’Eisan HaEzrachi* by our Rebbe, the Alter Rebbe, author of the Tanya and Shulchan Aruch.

This is especially true since the merit of the many stands with you—your activities in matters of Yiddishkeit, and first and foremost in the renovation of mikvaos, in addition to your books on *Mei HaDaas*. For there is no water but Torah, which is pure and stands forever; even words on the lips of human beings do not become tamei. This is particularly true of words from the "side of the Tree of Life," where there is no dispute from the spirit of tumah, etc.—the pnimiyus haTorah which has been revealed in our generation in the teachings of Chassidus.

May it be His will that just as we are commanded to increase in joy throughout this entire month, so too should it be in the affairs of each and every one, both in the details and in the general, from nefesh to basar.

As is known regarding the virtue of this month—the days of Purim—compared to the days of Chanukah, the matter of Purim includes the salvation of the body as well.

To conclude with a matter of the day, the 11th of the month, which is based on the beginning and end of our tractate, Maseches Megillah: "The Megillah is read on the 11th, etc., and on the 15th." It is known that this alludes to the Name Havayah, whose end is 11 (Vav-Hei) and whose beginning is 15 (Yud-Hei). The total sum of all the days of reading the Megillah, 11 through 15, is 65, the numerical value of the Name Adnus—the Name Havayah as it is read when it is concealed; i.e., when Elokus is hidden within the garments of the world. This is emphasized most strongly in the Megillah, where the Name is not mentioned explicitly, but only hinted at through acronyms and the final letters of words. Therefore, we are commanded that the Megillah must be **read**, and specifically with *pirsumei nisa*.

So too is it in general, through the study of Torah, which clarifies the matters of the world and decides and reveals that this is permitted, pure, and fit to be used to fulfill the will of Hashem and His mitzvos, and that this is not permitted for mundane work. Through this action of the **nefesh** of man **in the world**, one also reveals the true content and purpose of all the days of the **year**. For "every single day must do its work," and this is especially true through the special study that Moshe ordained for Yisrael, that they should inquire and expound **on the subject of the day**, and in the manner of Rashi’s commentary at the conclusion of the tractate—to accept and fulfill upon themselves and their children, in this world and the next.

With respect and blessing.

7597

B"H, 12 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, learned, and distinguished Rabbi Moshe, may he live, known as Dr. Munk,

Shalom and blessing!

In response to your letter of 20 Shevat and the one preceding it: Thank you for your thoughtfulness in writing to me regarding the Chabad educational institutions in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established. It is my hope that you will continue to do so in the future, and I thank you in advance. Certainly, you will continue to extend your assistance to all these institutions and their affiliates to the best of your ability, and in doing so, also influence, as much as lies within your power, other institutions and individuals who are in a position to act for the consolidation and development of these institutions. For one such as yourself, there is no need to elaborate on the magnitude of the matter of education specifically on the basis of purity and holiness.

Regarding the points you raise:

As for your comment that it is possible that the Chabad institutions demand too much from the students regarding academic standards, conduct, etc., and that perhaps there are those who will fear taking this yoke upon themselves, with the danger that they will go to an undesirable education, or at the very least, one not as desirable:

Beyond what you yourself write—that if the aforementioned requirements regarding academic standards and the like were to be lowered, it would also affect the other students, both the good and the average, such that the loss would likely outweigh the gain—there is another fundamental point here. The Chabad institutions possess a unique character, and the name itself teaches and conveys to all who hear that in this institution, one conducts oneself in a specific manner and studies at a certain level. To a most significant degree, all Chabad institutions throughout the entire world are uniform in this, at least in their fundamental principles. This means that lowering the standard and the level in one place forces consequences in this direction in other Chabad institutions, and not only within this country, but also outside of it.

There is another point here, based on a story I heard from my father-in-law, the Rebbe: There is a specific *hishtalshelus* (chain of progression) in the structure of human society. If, at a specific stage, a decline begins in one level, it is liable to cause a decline from level to level in all the stages that follow. Therefore, even if the decline at the first stage was from "the Holy of Holies to the holy," it will automatically cause a decline in the subsequent stages from "holy to mundane," and lower still—from "permitted to forbidden." My father-in-law, the Rebbe, explained through this how everyone, in their own standing and situation, must ascend in holiness, and certainly not descend, even if, after the descent, they would still remain elevated above their entire surroundings. The application to the aforementioned institutions is understood.

All of the above applies even if there were no specific custom in place for several years; all the more so when, for many years, they have conducted themselves in a specific manner, such that any decline in this is not at all worthwhile.

Especially since it is likely to be interpreted as a sign that "times have changed," customs are changing, and consequently, so are the requirements, etc. This is particularly true in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established, where in many desirable educational institutions, for various reasons, they do not demand from the students the same things you mention in your letter. Consequently, there is no danger, Heaven forbid, that candidates who are not accepted into Chabad institutions will be forced to be absorbed into an entirely different type of institution, for it is possible that they will be accepted into an institution whose curriculum simply does not include the aforementioned requirements.

Some time ago, I had a conversation similar to this, and I answered the questioner that there is an explicit instruction on this in the words of our Sages, of blessed memory, as is their way in a short but pointed adage: "A Rav found a breach and fenced it in." The meaning is: when a Rav saw that there was a matter being treated with neglect, he was stringent and added fences and decrees specifically in that matter. This means that when one sees that there is a circle where a breach exists in a certain matter—where, seemingly, there is a fear, as mentioned above, that if one is strict with them in this matter, they will be distanced entirely or pushed toward even worse matters—the instruction is to do the exact opposite: to urge them and be stringent with them specifically in the area that is breached. And the wise will understand.

2) Regarding the explanation to young men and women at the age of maturity concerning the subject and problems related to the *yetzer* and sexual life: It is difficult to establish a specific position on this, for despite the statement of our Sages on the verse you quote in your letter, "If there is anxiety in a man's heart, he should speak of it to others," it appears that in that same area, our Sages, of blessed memory, said: "A man has a small organ; if he starves it, it is satisfied, but if he satisfies it, it is hungry." Seemingly, the category of "satisfying it" also includes conversation about the aforementioned, unless the conversation is conducted by an expert who is most excellent at preventing certain expressions and associations. As I have seen in several places, the appropriate approach is that the teachers in the place should determine the conduct and the manner of conversation with a specific student (or a specific student), and at most, they should include two or three of them who are suited to one another—of course, in any case, boys separately and girls separately [but not in public, as is the custom in some places]. And even then, one must be very careful not to cause others to stumble or to stumble oneself in the prohibition [from the Torah, and at the very least, from the words of the Sages] of "do not bring yourself to thoughts of..." (*Shulchan Aruch, Even HaEzer* 23:3; and see *Tanya*, Chapter 11).

Here, I must express my deep regret that for several years, when the mass immigration of our Sephardic brothers to the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established, began, they found it "appropriate" to raise the age of marriage, contrary to what was practiced in the lands from which they came. And if, when the aforementioned decree was issued, there was doubt as to which was greater—the gain or the loss—the bitter results that this decree has brought over these years, Heaven forfend, and to our great sorrow, have proven the immense damage in this. It is simple that my intention is not to cry over the past, but rather that from time to time, opinions are renewed in the aforementioned area, whether to lower the age or to raise it, and my opinion is certainly understood from the above. Would that the Ashkenazim would also become accustomed to marriage at the youngest possible age, as per the words of Rav Chisda (*Kiddushin* 29b).

As we find ourselves on the eve of the days of Purim, a time of "and it was turned about," may it be the will that all confusing and negative matters be turned into light, joy, gladness, and honor, literally and as they are understood, according to the interpretation of our Sages, of blessed memory: "Light—this is Torah," etc.

With respect and with blessings for a happy Purim holiday.

## 7598

B"H, 12 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing, man of action, man of stature, descendant of holy lineage, etc., Rabbi Yitzchak Meir HaKohen, may he live.

a) Happy Purim. I have suggested to educational institutions that they endeavor to ensure that the boys and girls who are obligated in the mitzvos—and it is good that even younger children do so as well—personally fulfill the mitzvos of *mishloach manos* and *matanos la'evyonim*.

With respect, Menachem Schneerson

b) Thank you. In response to your telegram—in addition to the fulfillment of the mitzvos and the great educational value, the intention is a demonstration to the world below, and an emphasis, so to speak, to the world above, of the unity of our people, who are otherwise divided; and that this unity is only sustainable through the observance of our laws, which distinguish us from every other nation. Furthermore, it is to draw the youth—and through them, their parents—to walk in the ways of Mordechai HaYehudi, who rejected all forms of idolatry. With respect and blessing, Menachem Schneerson

[Letter from the Secretariat regarding the aforementioned proposal]

B"H, 7 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Rabbis, Yeshiva heads, educators, teachers, and parents, may Hashem grant them life.

Peace and blessing!

In connection with the upcoming days of Purim, we have been instructed by the Rebbe shlita to draw general attention to the unfortunately widespread misunderstanding regarding the mitzvos of **mishloach manos** and **matanos la'evyonim** that prevails among Jewish children and youth. Many of them rely on the assumption that they can fulfill these mitzvos through their parents performing them, and unfortunately, it often happens that many parents do not fulfill them as they should either.

Therefore, with the proper respect, the Rabbis, educators, and parents are requested to remind the children who are under their guidance and influence of the following:

a) Along with the mitzvah to hear the Megillah on Purim night and the following morning, there is an obligation upon every boy from the age of thirteen and up, and every girl from the age of twelve and up, to **personally** fulfill the mitzvos of **mishloach manos** and **matanos la'evyonim** on the day of Purim.

Note: From an educational standpoint, it is desirable that younger children also be trained to fulfill these mitzvos.

b) These mitzvos can be easily carried out. For **mishloach manos**, only two types of food are required (e.g., an apple and a candy, or a piece of cake and a soda, or juice); **matanos la'evyonim** can be fulfilled with two cents, one cent for each of two poor people.

In the certain hope that this matter will find the proper resonance to bring merit to the many, and with a heartfelt wish for a joyous and spiritually rich Purim.

With respect and blessing,

Secretariat of the Rebbe shlita

## 7,599

B"H, 17 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from Motzaei Shabbos Parshas Tetzaveh, in which you write regarding the apartment exchange and that, G-d willing, you are returning to your apartment in the new village, and regarding your thought on this matter based on what is written in Sefer Chassidim, etc.

This is not applicable to your specific case, for your son lived there as well—*d'vara kara d'avuh* (the son is like the leg of the father)—and furthermore, it was a condition that you (the writer) would return after a short time. And above all, the entire village, from its inception and in its management, is under the authority of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, Nasi Yisroel.

In general, regarding matters such as these, it is sufficient for us to rely on the established custom, and one should not add [stringencies]. However, since you have raised the point, it is appropriate that before you return to your apartment, you should arrange for public learning to take place there for at least three days, once each day, as a *chazakah* is established by three times and three days. After that, you may dwell there in tranquility, joy, and goodness of heart.

I trust you are keeping the three daily *shiurim*—Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya—as is well known. It is also appropriate to have your Tefillin checked, as well as the Mezuzos of your current residence, and to affix them in the aforementioned apartment.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above.

**Regarding the apartment exchange:** In his letter: "When the construction of the new village was completed, I moved from my apartment in the old village to the new village. After my son’s marriage... I gave him my apartment in the new village on the condition that when the housing units were built, he would move to the housing unit and I would return to the new village, at which point I moved back to the old apartment in the old village. Now that I hope, G-d willing, to return to my apartment in the new village, I found it written in Sefer Chassidim: 'One who dwells in a house and leaves it should not return to dwell in it until after seven full years.'"

## 7600

B"H, 17 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, learned, and distinguished, etc., R' Moshe, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

After a long interval, I received your letter dated 10 Adar. It gave me pleasure to read at the end of your letter that you will endeavor to disseminate the teachings of Chassidus, and specifically Chassidus Chabad, in public and with breadth. If you will, this is also connected to these days, as your letter was written in the days preceding Purim and received in the days immediately following Purim—a time when all the matters and miracles involved wine. This is explained at length in *Likutei Torah* by our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, author of the *Tanya* and *Shulchan Aruch* (Shir HaShirim 26:3 and onward). May it be the will [of Hashem] that you act in this regard in a manner that is ever-increasing.

## 7002

B"H, 18 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn

To the esteemed and distinguished individual, occupied with communal needs, R' Mattisyahu, may he live:

Peace and blessing!

I was pleased to receive the news regarding your efforts to maintain the minyan in the shul three times a day, and in general, to strengthen the affairs of the shul in many details.

And it gave me special joy to hear the news that you are going to install a bimah in the center of the shul, just as shuls were in the "old country" for generations.

And as is explained in many places in the holy seforim, the extreme importance of the tikkun of having the bimah in the center of the shul.

For if in every place and in every matter, a Yid must do everything so that it is in full accordance with the will of Hashem Yisbarach, Creator of the world, with the greatest possible hidur, then this must certainly be so to the maximum degree in a shul, which Hashem Yisbarach calls "a place where I cause My name to be mentioned," and where Yidden come to ask of Hashem Yisbarach—for themselves and for all their loved ones and friends—for everything they are in need of, and they want Hashem Yisbarach to listen to the tefillah and fulfill the request.

It is therefore understandable that this must be done in such a way and condition that it is in accordance with the will of the One from Whom one is coming to request the petition, and it should be as mehudar as possible, to find as much favor and grace as possible with the One who can fulfill the request.

And may Hashem Yisbarach help that everything should be carried out with much success, with joy and a good heart.

And especially since we are in the days of the month of Adar, regarding which our Sages of blessed memory have instructed, "increase in joy," and an instruction from Torah is simultaneously a granting of strength and the ability to fulfill that instruction.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above, and with a warm regard to all those who are exerting themselves and will add to the efforts regarding the affairs of the shul, that it should fully earn the name "mikdash me'at" and with honor.

## 7,303

B"H, 18 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 10 Adar.

At a time of favor, I will mention all those you wrote about at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, in accordance with the content of your letter. May it be His will that just as you have written your request, so too may you bring good news regarding it.

Regarding what you wrote about growing a beard and that some suggest "trimming" it:

It is unclear what you mean by the word "trimming." In my opinion—based on what you wrote—there is no room to cut it, even to trim it. If one must reflect carefully on how to discontinue a good custom that one has practiced for some time, how much more so is this the case here, where the Tzemach Tzedek rules that this is not merely a good custom, but an obligation. For someone who has conducted himself according to the instruction of the **Rebbe**—it is incomprehensible and would raise great astonishment if he were to suddenly change this. Perhaps you mean "straightening" it with a comb (although there are select individuals who are meticulous and careful about this, and in the language used—they did not touch the hair of their beards). As mentioned, only select individuals practiced this, and one may straighten the beard with a comb. In this way, you will fulfill the mitzvah of honoring your mother as discussed.

As for the concern that perhaps the beard hinders matters of respectability—it is astonishing that such a thought would even arise, since the opinion of Chazal is explicit, **without any dispute**, that the thirteen *tikunei dikna* (rectifications of the beard) are channels for *hashpa'ah* (divine influence) and the receiving of blessings from above. It is obvious that these only assist in desirable matters, especially regarding a truly good *shidduch*.

May it be His will that you bring good news regarding all of the above, and I also confirm that you have reassured your mother, may she live and be well, with pleasant ways and explanatory words, in accordance with what was written above.

As you requested, the letter is being sent without a queue.

With blessings for good news in all the above,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

## 7404

B"H, 19 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter, which arrived with a delay, in which you write about the situation in your family, particularly regarding health matters, etc., and ask for my opinion on this.

In general, it is puzzling that you write as if a doctor said there is no chance for a full recovery, at a time when there are several known cases that have clearly recovered completely. Among them are those whom I have seen myself, and it has been more than ten years that they have been fine; furthermore, in the meantime, they have married (for the person I am referring to, whom I have known throughout this entire time, was single, married, and has had children) and earns a steady living.

Regarding a specific event, it is impossible to know clearly and definitively, but simply put, a doctor’s statement in the style mentioned is highly questionable. At most, and generally, a person can only say that he does not guarantee and does not take responsibility regarding the future, and nothing more than that.

In general, regarding the matter as you describe it, and especially since there are children who are at the age of chinuch, it is obvious that one must endeavor to seek every possible means so that the home continues to exist.

Furthermore, and most importantly, one must remain in constant contact with specialists in this field, to prompt them and ask them periodically about methods of treatment and medications that are frequently being developed, to see if any are suitable in this case as well.

May Hashem, who oversees each and every one with Divine Providence, and who is also the Healer of all flesh and performs wonders, guide you and each of those mentioned in your letter toward what is truly good for them—a good that is visible and revealed, which is good for Heaven and good for people.

May it be His will that there be a fundamental change for the better in this immediately; after all, we are in the month of Adar, days that are successful for the Jewish people in all their affairs.

Surely you do not content yourself with the shiurim you write and add to from time to time, and surely you also participate in shiurim that are studied in public.

Regarding your question about the permission, etc.—it is understood that for this, you must turn to a qualified Rav on the spot who knows all the details, etc.

With blessings for good tidings.

#### ***7404***

**The Situation:** Nervous disorder, and there is a danger to the children because of it.

## Z'tara

B"H, 21 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

...I do not know the custom in Jerusalem (may it be rebuilt and established) among various circles regarding the erecting of a *matzeivah*, but the conclusion of several later *poskim* is well-known: that one should not delay it, and certainly one should not wait until the completion of a year or the like.

And from one matter to a related matter: the instruction of our Sages is wondrous, that immediately upon the passing of a person from Israel, it is specifically their relatives who must endeavor that, in connection with the passing, *Yisgadal v’yiskadash shmei raba* (and not only in the heavens above, but) *b’alma di-vera chir’usei*—in the world that He created according to His will—be recited. Surely, the intention of this recitation is both for the sake of the departed and for the sake of the living (may they live long and good years); it is a merit for the *neshamah* and a consolation for the mourners. And if you would ask—by way of explanation and response to the natural protest, "How can this be?"—the world and its conduct are indeed *chir’usei*, the will of the Creator of the world. It is no wonder at all that the human intellect, which is limited to the extreme, has no grasp of the Divine Will, for "Can you find the depths of Elokus?"

With blessings for your personal matters and also for general matters—by which I mean the dissemination of traditional Judaism. You must now engage in this twofold, and such an obligation is also a merit, a merit as well for the *neshamah*, which is eternal, and for good tidings in revealed goodness.

The booklet was received, and many thanks for the thoughtfulness of sending it here.

#### ***Z'tara***

**Regarding the erecting of a *matzeivah*:** See also Vol. 17, Letter 6,319, and the references noted in the footnotes there.

## 770

B"H, 24 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn.

To the avreich, Shlomo, may he live:

Greetings and blessings!

After a very long interval, your letter of 6 Adar was received. In the meantime, a request was sent to you from the secretariat urging the fulfillment of the mitzvah of sending *mishloach manos* and *matanos la'evyonim*, especially among the youth. Beyond the inherent greatness of the mitzvah itself (to the point that our Sages taught that there is no reward for a mitzvah in this world, for the entire world cannot contain it), this has a uniquely important educational value. I look forward to hearing good news regarding what was accomplished in this regard.

Regarding your writing about *matzah shemurah*, I will endeavor to assist with this, and I am certain you will confirm receipt. If I am successful, I will send—in addition to your request—a few extra *matzos*, and it is my hope that you will distribute them among those you influence, while explaining the magnitude and importance of the matter, especially based on what is brought and explained in many places regarding the essence of the matter: that *matzah* is called the "food of faith" (*michla d'meheimanusa*) and the "food of healing" (*michla d'asvusa*).

With blessings for good news in all the aforementioned, and—most importantly—that you utilize all your strengths, possibilities, and capabilities to disseminate traditional Judaism, and primarily—at its core—the fulfillment of *mitzvos ma'asiyos* **in practice**, in every circle and place where your influence reaches.

In response to your questions: 
a) It is understood that the point and purpose of the study is for the student to know and understand the content of the *pasuk*, not a mechanical translation from language to language. Especially since knowing the content reminds and strengthens the knowledge of the translation of the words. 
b) In most periods, and especially now when study hours are extremely limited, in schools of the type you write about, one must utilize **every** hour and moment for the necessary and vital studies, i.e., the study of the laws of Yisrael that are **practical in the life** of the student and the family, the fundamentals of faith (the thirteen "I believe" principles), the *parsha* of the week (or *Chumash* in order), the explanation of the words in *tefillah*, selected chapters from *Nevi'im* and *Kesuvim* (naturally, this includes the entire *Megillas Esther*, *Rus*, *Eichah*), selected *Mishnayos*, and excerpts from *Ein Yaakov* and *Gemara*. *Mussar* and *Chassidus* should be included in the study of one of the above (in the explanation of the fundamentals of faith, the explanation of *tefillah*, and the like).

I look forward to good news.

#### ***770***

**Shlomo:** Rubel, Buenos Aires.

**From the secretariat:** Copied above—7598.

## 7008

B.H., 25 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Rabbi, Chassid, G-d-fearing man, man of integrity, activist,
A man of many accomplishments, a man of stature, from a distinguished lineage, etc.
Rabbi Yitzchak Meir, may he live, Hakohen

Greetings and blessings!

I received your letter of 11 Adar with thanks. Surely, in the meantime, you have also received my second telegram in response to the question posed in your telegram.

You will certainly be pleased to know that the call regarding *mishloach manos* and *matanos la'evyonim* found a proper echo, fitting the words of our Sages that "action is the essential thing." I dare say that it appears many thousands of youth fulfilled these *mitzvos* for the first time in their lives (thanks to the aforementioned awakening). It is truly wondrous how sensitive the heart of a Yid is; through a mere announcement, they fulfill a good deed. This provides additional encouragement to all those who have influence over others, that they should not, Heaven forbid, be like a "voice calling in the wilderness," etc. Even if at times—and perhaps frequently—the results are not immediately visible, or even visible after some time, in the phrasing of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe: "It is a *chazakah* that propaganda [efforts] do not return empty."

It is understood and simple that, for the time being, it is easier to arouse people to the actual fulfillment of these specific *mitzvos* than those involving greater difficulties, etc. But surely, "one *mitzvah* leads to another," preparing the soul of the one who performs it so that it will, at the very least, be easier to arouse him to fulfill yet another *mitzvah* and another.

Certainly, for one such as yourself, who is on the scene, it is superfluous to emphasize how the above is timely, given the current situation in the Holy Land (may it be rebuilt and established)—the lack of unity on the left, reaching alarming proportions. This underscores in actual practice the truth of what is said, "Do not call it a conspiracy," etc., for surely, such a conspiracy has no endurance. How sweet are the words of our *Alter Rebbe* in the *Tanya Kadisha*, Chapter 32. My hope is strong that you will not withhold the favor of informing me regarding the continuation of the above, as well as the general situation regarding *Yiddishkeit*, etc., in your vicinity and surroundings.

And from one subject to a similar one: these days I received photos from São Paulo, including some of your participation in the *farbrengen* for the "Day of the News," 19 Kislev, at the Oholei Yosef Yitzchak institution there.

Apparently, for some reason, this information was delayed until now. We "roll" good and joyful news toward the happy days of the month of Adar, in which we increase in joy.

With respect and blessing for good tidings—good for Heaven, good for people, and good for the one who produces fruit—with joy and a good heart.

## 7059

B"H, 25 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To all participants in the Kinus of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad Mid-West, Erev Rosh Chodesh Nissan – 2 Nissan, may they live:

Shalom u’vracha!

Two of the foundations of the Baal Shem Tov’s teachings, which are explained extensively in Chassidus Chabad, are that every detail and event in human life is by Hashgacha Pratis, and that from every thing one must learn a directive in Avodas Hashem.

Especially an event as important as the gathering of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad Mid-West, and the fact that it takes place on the Yahrtzeit-Hilula of the Rebbe Rashab, the father of my father-in-law, the Rebbe — ztzkllh"h, nbg"m, zy"a — and on Erev Rosh Chodesh and Rosh Chodesh Nissan.

I wish to remind all participants of this Kinus of the holy words of the Baal HaHilula — for all the words of the Nesi’ei Yisrael, especially those that were spoken in public and were printed, have an eternal value, significance, and effect:

"All the nations of the world must know that only our bodies were delivered into exile and the subjugation of kingdoms, but our souls were not driven into exile and were not delivered into the subjugation of kingdoms. We must state clearly for all to see, that as far as our religion, Torah, Mitzvos, and Jewish customs are concerned, we Jews have no one to dictate to us."

The Baal HaHilula embodied this life-outlook and carried it out into daily life, both under the Czarist regime, which was known for its decrees and persecutions of Jews, and in the years of the Communist regime with its anti-religious system in general, and anti-Jewish religion in particular.

This is also the fundamental idea of the month of Nissan, the month of Geulah. By virtue of the fact that the Jews in the exile of Mitzrayim — the first exile and the prototype of **all** subsequent exiles — did not adopt the ways of the Egyptians, they remained distinct there, guarding their separateness, difference, and uniqueness — the Geulah of Mitzrayim and Yetzias Mitzrayim came about.

All the more so, when we find ourselves in a land of tranquility and religious freedom, it is our duty and also our privilege to strengthen and expand true Yiddishkeit, Torah, Mitzvos, and Jewish customs, illuminated and enlivened according to the directives of Toras Chassidus Chabad, and this depends solely on our will.

**Mozes** (you can) — it only needs to be **Chatzesh** (that you should want).

May it be the will of Hashem that the Kinus fulfill its tasks in actual practice, and to the fullest extent, in accordance with the inner intention and the explicit instruction of our holy Rebbes, whose merit and the merit of the public will surely assist everyone toward exceptional success in communal work, and consequently also in one’s own personal matters, both materially and spiritually together.

With blessings for success and good tidings.

## 7,609*

B"H, 25 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed and distinguished individual, occupied with the needs of the community, whose work is the work of Heaven,
R' Shmuel, may he live,

Greetings and blessings!

...Regarding what you wrote, that you are not receiving assistance, etc.: First and foremost, it is certain that for every one of those working in the holy task of chinuch—pure and untainted—and especially within the framework of our Rebbeim, the Nesi'im, the verse "My help comes from Hashem, Maker of heaven and earth" is fulfilled in practice. [This can be interpreted as follows: "Maker" (*oseh*) is related to the language of *asiyah* (action) and *kofia* (coercion)—that Hashem coerces and overturns the concealments and hiddenness within nature, until they are transformed into sources of assistance. This is similar to the concept of the days of Purim, when the common people became Jews, etc., and "it was overturned," etc. And surely, for someone like you, who has seen this tangibly in the recent past, there is no need to elaborate or bring proofs for such a simple matter.]

It is my hope that what you wrote at the end of your letter regarding yourself—is consistent with what was written above—that the help from Hashem has "arrived" for you as well, and is performing its work.

May it be the will of Hashem that as we stand at the conclusion and seal of the month of Adar, when we are commanded to increase in joy, and on the eve of the month of Nissan, the month of redemption from all constraints and boundaries, this will be reflected in your holy work, at Beis Rivkah and in your profession. May you bring good news—good in a way that is visible even to physical eyes—until you are able to conduct yourself in accordance with the verse, "And I will walk in a broad place"—in all matters of the institution, and with expansion in your personal matters as well.

This also serves as an additional response to the conclusion of your letter regarding the move, etc. It is similar to a story I heard from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, about a Chossid of the Alter Rebbe, R' Mordechai Lepler, who would defeat his *yetzer* with the argument: How is it possible for him to listen to its enticements when everyone knows that he is wealthy and has access to the royal courts—to the powerful monarchy of Russia in those days—and first and foremost, that he is a Chossid of the Alter Rebbe...

## 7610

B"H, 27 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn

Blessings and peace!

Following a very long hiatus, your letter from 10 Adar was received. As you requested, I am sending this response without waiting for the regular turn.

In it, you write about the problems with your eldest son, may he live, and how to influence him, etc.

It is understood that there are no fixed rules in a situation such as this, as every case differs from the next; people’s opinions and character traits are not the same. It also depends on how far things have progressed [in the dialogue between the two individuals]. However, the common denominator in any situation is that one succeeds far more through pleasant, gentle ways than through the opposite approach. Furthermore, combining two issues—marriage and education for a religious life—is extremely difficult, as I am referring to changing the other party's worldview alongside the marriage itself. In any event, it is essential that she conduct herself with religious observance in her daily life for a period of time before the marriage, so that she at least understands the burden involved and the difficulties of a lifestyle to which she was not previously accustomed. In the language of our Sages, a person cannot commit to or take on a matter whose essence and nature they do not know.

At a time of favor, I will mention all those you wrote about at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, in accordance with the content of your letter. May it be His will that you bring good news regarding each and every one of them.

I have a duty to point out—though it is unpleasant, especially since I have already hinted at this in my previous letter to your husband, may he live—that this phenomenon, as well as others you mentioned in your letter, is in all likelihood a result of his being in educational institutions that are **not** in accordance with what you and your husband, may he live, had hoped for.

Regrettably, it is human nature to be **most** prone to the following error: when parents hope for a certain level of worldview and religious conduct from their children, they educate them in a way—almost strictly—that ensures they will not reach a level higher than, or even equal to, the status and standing of the parents themselves. They do not take into account at all that if a person in middle age maintains a certain religious standing, it is only because in their youth and adolescence they stood at a higher level. Even though the hardships of life and the battle for survival (and the environment as well) diminished the level of their youth, they remained at least at the level of their middle age. From this, it is understood that a person who stands at a diminished level during their youth will naturally, after a few years, descend from that standing and find themselves at a level lower than what was hoped for.

The conclusion from the above is that if you and your husband, may he live, wanted the practical *yiras shamayim* of your children to be equal to your own, the children should have been educated in an institution that demands from its students the same measure of *yiras shamayim* that *Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim* demanded of your husband. But when children are educated in an institution like the one mentioned in your letter—where, according to reports, the level of *yiras shamayim* required is **many** degrees lower than that required by *Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim* and its likes—what wonder is it when they descend to a level lower than that, and these events that you write about become possible?

It is understood that I do not intend to preach, but rather to speak regarding your other children, may they live, who are still under your care. It is simple that they must learn and be educated within the four cubits of an institution that demands a complete measure of *yiras shamayim* from its students. Then, one can demand and hope for all matters of Torah and *mitzvos* from the children, since the parents have given them every possibility for this and they have never been in an environment whose attitude is lukewarm, etc.

In recent years, in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established, many parents have changed their attitude and are acting in the aforementioned direction—that is, to enroll their sons and daughters in a *yeshiva* where they study only *limudei kodesh*, or in *Beis Yaakov*, *Beis Rivkah*, and the like. This movement is spreading from time to time. If this is true for everyone, it is all the more so for those (you and your husband, may he live) who see with their own eyes the results of education in state schools, and even in schools that are one level above them, etc.

It is not my place to elaborate on this painful matter, as it does not speak to the welfare of Israel.

It is clear that everything stated above is directed toward your husband, may he live, as well. I have a strong hope that you will not be offended by my repeating the above; the reason is that I feel it is my duty not to remain silent, since you wrote to me about the situation.

With blessings for good news,

**M. Schneerson**

P.S. Your husband’s letter, may he live, was received, and the response is included in the above.

## 6,711

B"H, 28 Adar, 5721
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings!

It was with pleasure that I received the news regarding your entry into the yoke of mitzvos.

I send you my heartfelt blessings that you may take upon yourselves the yoke of Torah and the yoke of its mitzvos with joy and a glad heart. For although this is a matter of *kabbalas ol*—and a complete *kabbalas ol* at that, just as it was when our entire people, the Children of Israel, came under the yoke of Torah and mitzvos at the standing at Har Sinai, at the time when they preceded "we will do" to "we will hear"—nevertheless, it is a pleasant yoke. For there is no good other than Torah and its mitzvos, as it is written: "For I have given you a good acquisition; do not forsake My Torah." And the study of Torah leads to the action of fulfilling the mitzvos in daily life.

And in accordance with what is stated, "If you walk in My statutes and keep My mitzvos... then I will give..." and all the blessings enumerated in that *parsha*, may it be the will of Hashem that you conduct yourselves in accordance with the above as is required, and may the Ribono shel Olam fulfill His promise that you be blessed with everything necessary, in both material and spiritual matters alike.

With blessings for success in the study of Torah and the fulfillment of its mitzvos.

On the contrary, it seems that source provides proof to the **contrary**, since it was **upheld** specifically according to the words of Rabbi Akiva, that he is a *mamzer* and the child of a *niddah*, and they said that Rabbi Akiva was great because he **refuted** the words of those who disagreed with him on this.

## 7613

[Adar 5721]

It is astonishing: people talk and talk, yet when it comes to action—everyone thinks the intention is for someone else, not for themselves!

Many times, the **instruction** of our Rebbes, the Nesi’im, has been cited: *Tracht gut, vet zein gut*—"Think good, and it will be good" (meaning that the thought itself will **bring about** this result). Yet, in contrast to this, people "invent" thoughts of an opposite nature. Furthermore, even though we are in the month of Adar, when we are commanded to increase in joy, they remain mired in sadness (see Tanya regarding this), and so on.

And we conclude with the *Dvar Malchus*: *Tracht gut, vet zein gut*—in a revealed and manifest way.

#### ***7613***

Published in *Likutei Sichos*, Vol. 21, p. 428

## 7614

B"H, 2 Nissan, the day of the histalkus hilula of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe (the Rebbe Rashab),
Brooklyn, N.Y.
5721. The centennial year of his birth, may his merit shield us.

May Hashem grant them life,

Shalom and blessings!

As we approach the festival of Matzos, the time of our freedom, which is coming upon us and all of Yisrael for good, I hereby express my blessing to each and every one of them — a blessing for freedom from all matters that distract from the avodah of Hashem with joy and a glad heart, and for a kosher and joyous holiday.

For joy breaks through boundaries; one who conducts himself in matters of Torah and mitzvos above his own limitations and nature receives guidance from Above — the boundaries of the world are broken through for his sake, and he is blessed with children, life, and sustenance (in all these matters) in an abundant manner.

In accordance with the saying of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, that the festival of Pesach continues and shines every day and shines always — may it be His will that this be so in all their personal and communal matters alike, and throughout all the days of the year,

With blessings for a kosher and joyous Pesach and for a healthy and happy summer.

Sh. Z. shlita, the Mara D'Asra, at their head, distribute it to the residents of Kfar Chabad and to the select Chabad activists in Eretz Yisrael, may it be rebuilt speedily — shlita.

## 7,415

B"H, 4 Nissan, 5721
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

Following a very long hiatus, your letter of 28 Adar has been received.

At an opportune time, I will mention you at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, in accordance with the contents of your letter. May it be His will that just as you have written your request, so too may you bring good tidings regarding everything mentioned in your letter. This also applies to your activities in spreading the wellsprings of Chassidus; this has no connection whatsoever to the Talmudic dictum that "a prisoner cannot free himself from prison," for there is no "prison" situation at all, Heaven forbid. This is certainly true, and even more so, compared to our country in the past—where even then, regarding this matter, such a situation did not exist—and all the more so in these countries. On the contrary, we see tangibly that when one makes even the slightest effort in the aforementioned dissemination, especially in these years, it succeeds to an ever-greater degree. It depends on nothing but one’s will.

It goes without saying that this is not written as a rebuke, Heaven forbid, but rather as an expression of sorrow that anash—and especially those whose noble lineage assists them—do not utilize, in any way, the vast possibilities granted to them in this regard. This is particularly true since this also adds to one’s joy and goodness of heart, and consequently, to physical health in the literal sense as well. And this is easy to understand.

As we stand in the month of Nissan and approach the festival of Pesach, the time of our freedom, may you experience freedom from all distracting matters in all the affairs mentioned in your letter, and may you bring good tidings.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach.

## 7716

B"H, 6 Nissan, 5721
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 3 Nissan regarding your state of mind and the thoughts that are troubling you.

It would be appropriate for you to look into the Sefer Tanya Kadisha by our Rebbe, the Baal HaTanya (the authority on the hidden dimensions of Torah), and the Shulchan Aruch (the authority on the revealed dimensions of Torah), regarding what is written there concerning the negation of sadness, etc. (consult the subject index in the Kehot edition of Tanya).

Furthermore, and this is the main point: you should contemplate the concept of Hashgacha Pratis—how Hashem oversees each and every individual with specific providence, and that He is the essence of good, and it is the nature of the Good to bestow good. From this, the immediate result and conclusion is that which was expressed by the Sweet Singer of Israel: "Hashem is my shepherd, I shall not lack... I will fear no evil."

In general, it is not worthwhile to waste your time contemplating your own state of mind. It is far better to study Torah Shebichtav and Torah Shebaal Peh in a way that does not strain you, as mentioned above, in a manner that will not cause you fatigue until your state of mind improves completely.

Regarding what you wrote about the advantage you feel when davening in private: many who are in a similar situation follow the practice of beginning to daven with the tzibbur, even if their own davening takes longer afterward and they continue in private.

It is appropriate that you have your tefillin checked, as well as the mezuzos in your home, to ensure they are all kosher according to the law. Furthermore, every weekday before morning davening, set aside a few coins for tzedakah. It is appropriate that you keep up with the three daily study cycles: Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya.

With blessings for good tidings, in joy and goodness of heart, and a kosher and happy Pesach.

#### ***7716***

**Regarding what you wrote:** See also the previous letter, 7660, and the references in the footnotes there.

## 719

719

*[7 Nissan 5721]*

I say "somewhat," because I hope that you are indeed active in this; nevertheless, the lack of information certainly detracts from the effect, as is tangibly evident. May it be Hashem’s will that you bring good news regarding this as well. After all, according to the *Shulchan Aruch*, the true definition of "good" in such a matter is not merely doing the act, but doing it with the intent of one who truly desires to perform it.

## 7220

B"H, 10 Nissan, 5721
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Mr. Reuven Avinoam,

Shalom u’Bracha,

I hereby acknowledge with thanks receipt of your book of poetry, "Etz Shatalti."

It is surely unnecessary to elaborate on the subject of the eternity of the soul, which is not only a fundamental principle of our faith, but also a matter acceptable to human reason—especially in our current era, where it is an accepted scientific law that nothing is ever lost, not even the most physical and material things. How much more so is this true regarding that which gives life to matter and physicality: the soul.

From this follows the conclusion that the soul, upon being liberated from the limitations of the body and the conditions of this physical and material world, is able to express its inherent spirituality and purity—which are aspects that the body, the flesh, obscures, and which require a struggle to allow the soul to prevail over the body—to a fuller extent and in a more refined manner.

A further consequence of the above is that all activities that provide satisfaction to the soul while it is still in the body—where the intent is surely the inwardness and spirituality of the soul, rather than the incidental needs of the perishable body—have a place even after the soul has departed from the body. Indeed, they do so with even greater intensity and strength, for two reasons: firstly, because the essential qualities of the soul are more revealed in and of themselves, without concealments and obstructions; and secondly, due to the attachment of spirit to spirit, where the spirituality and light of these actions reach the soul without the mediation of the physical senses.

From the above, it is also understood why our faith and our Torah—the Torah of life—limits mourning for the departed to specific times, and at the same time demands that just as mourning has its proper time and place, so too is mourning forbidden when it is not the proper time. We see tangibly that mourning diminishes energy and vitality, yet the essential thing is the deed—the good actions, their continuation, and their persistence—and, on the contrary, adding to them and ascending in holiness. This affects not only the one performing the deeds, but also the soul of the departed.

May it be the will of Hashem that actions in the spirit of our eternal Torah, which is also a Torah of life—a guide for life in this world—illuminate his path before him, and that he may also influence his surroundings in the direction mentioned above. This will certainly and surely bring the greatest possible satisfaction to the soul of the departed as well, and may we soon merit the fulfillment of the promise, "And Hashem will wipe away tears from off all faces."

With respect and blessing, and with the blessing of a kosher and happy Pesach.

## 7281

7281

B"H, 10 Nissan, 5721
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter, in which you write various reasonings regarding the manner of continuing your studies in connection with the difficulties of the current arrangement therein.

The reasoning you write in your letter is very appropriate: that you should study in the class for an entire day of *limudei kodesh*, and also continue with *limudei kodesh* afterwards, as you write, for an hour or an hour and a half with someone as a *rebbe*. However, it is also very necessary that even after that, you should have a *chavrusa*, or even an older student, with whom to review what has already been learned and to know it well, and not to study new material.

And may Hashem Yisbarach grant you success to be among the first to demonstrate in Melbourne, and in Australia in general, to *anash* and also to all our brethren, the Children of Israel, that a *bochur*—whom every Jewish child is called a son of Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov—must study at least several years exclusively *limudei kodesh*. Beyond the fact that this places him on the proper path and prepares him to be happy in life, not only in *ruchniyus* but also in *gashmiyus*, it also brings *nachas* from him—true *nachas*—to his parents, good friends, and the *hanhalah* of the *yeshiva* included.

Regarding what you write about going to study in a *yeshiva* in *Eretz HaKodesh* (may it be rebuilt and established) or here:

After you maintain the aforementioned schedule while in Melbourne for a year or two—which will also solidify the aforementioned conduct, that a Jewish *bochur* must conduct himself in this manner—then your request, as mentioned above, will be taken into consideration.

May it be His will that you do this with *simcha* and a good heart, and may your parents (may they live and be well) have much true *nachas* from you, which is Jewish, Chassidishe *nachas*.

With blessings for good tidings and for a *kosher* and happy *Chag Pesach*.

## 7,222

B"H, 10 Nissan, 5721
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 22 Adar, which was received in due course, in which you write regarding the ailment of your wife, may she live.

There is room to suggest that perhaps not all the mezuzos are kosher, or that they were not affixed according to halacha. In any event, she should completely dismiss the dreams from her mind, for they have no substance. As our Sages of blessed memory stated, dreams neither elevate nor diminish. She should strengthen herself in conduct appropriate to the instructions of our Torah, the Torah of life, and in the fulfillment of its mitzvos, regarding which it is said, "and he shall live by them." May Hashem grant you success in the fulfillment of your hearts' desires for the good, and to the extent that you increase your trust in Hashem and in the aforementioned conduct, so too will the blessing increase.

With blessings for good tidings in all the aforementioned.

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 7263

By the Grace of Hashem, 11 Nissan, 5721
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To our brethren, the Children of Israel, wherever they may be,
May Hashem protect them and grant them life,

Greetings and blessings!

The Exodus from Egypt—the focal point of the festival of Pesach—occupies a central place in the life of the Jew, both for the community and for the individual. The departure from Egypt instructs the Jew and serves as a guide, not only through its general content, but also through its specific details.

One of the primary characteristics of the Exodus from Egypt is the extraordinary *bitachon* in Hashem and His Divine Providence, which was expressed in the following way: An entire nation, millions of people—men, women, and children—suddenly abandoned a settled land, a land blessed with every material necessity, and set out on a long journey without food or supplies, relying solely and completely on the words of Moshe Rabbeinu in the name of Hashem.

Moreover, in leaving Egypt, they did not take the known, shorter route through the land of the Philistines. Even though that route was fraught with the danger of war, in a war there is always a possibility of victory; and even if not, there is at least a chance that some would survive. Instead, they went into a desolate wilderness, where there was no natural possibility of survival.

And once again, the only reason for doing so was that this is what Moshe had instructed in the name of Hashem.

The wonder is even greater when one considers that this took place after the Jews had lived in that same location for many years without any wandering. Furthermore, this was in Goshen, Egypt—a land that possessed the best, most fertile soil for sowing and growing, a land that did not depend on rain but was always assured of water for irrigation and drinking from the Nile River—a land entirely established and living according to the laws and circumstances of "nature."

* * *

From the days of your departure from the land of Egypt—ever since the Jews left Egypt—the aforementioned [concept] **must** be the unconditional companion and guide for the Jew, for the individual and for the community, in all times: the absolute *bitachon* in Hashem’s Providence and conducting oneself in all matters, even in daily life, according to His instructions, regardless of what human calculations and the laws of nature dictate.

**This** provides the answer to the question that the non-Jewish world, and the "non-Jewish" elements among the Jews themselves, ask of Jews who are *shomrei Torah u'mitzvos*:

We live in a world of competition and the struggle for existence; everyone lives in a specific country with certain circumstances—so how can one refuse to bow down and serve the idols of the land (whether this is the dollar, or the fear of being different from one's neighbors, etc.)? How can one bind oneself with 613 *mitzvos* that "burden" and restrict a person's activity and work at every step?

The answer is—the Exodus from Egypt. And as explained earlier:

Just as at the time of the Exodus, following Hashem’s instructions—without regard for the calculations of the circumstances, etc., and breaking away from the past of many years in Egypt—was precisely the path to true happiness, not only to spiritual happiness (receiving the Torah and becoming the chosen people of Hashem), but also to material happiness (receiving a land flowing with milk and honey),

So too today and always, regardless of what the past may have been, for every Jew and for *Klal Yisrael*, the only path to true happiness is organizing one's daily life—and life in general—according to the Torah and its *mitzvos*, of which it is written, "and he shall live by them."

* * *

Jews have been commanded: "In all your ways, know Him." In all his activities, throughout his entire day, a Jew has the instructions of the Torah and must follow them.

Therefore, he may at any time encounter the aforementioned question and challenge. And for this reason, everyone has been commanded: "You shall remember the day of your departure from the land of Egypt—**all the days of your life.**" A remembrance that instructs, illuminates, and gives strength for a conduct that ensures all days are truly "Jewish" living days. And as my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, emphasized: The festival of Pesach illuminates and descends not only on each day, but—constantly.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach,

**Menachem Schneerson**

## 7,224

B"H, 13 Nissan, 5721
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Mr. Tzvi Gat
Greetings and blessings!

After the longest of intervals, your letter was received from your new office location in Senegal, and I read with interest about the details of your work there. It is my hope that wherever you visit—if our brethren, the Children of Israel, are to be found there, and seemingly there is no country or city where there are not some of the Children of Israel—you endeavor to meet with them, to remind them, to awaken them, and to encourage them regarding the rock from which they were hewn, etc., etc.

For we do not always know in our actions in this world what is the main point, to the extent that it is possible that the Cause of all causes brings about many reasons, actions, and matters for the sake of a single event, a single meeting, or a single detail. We see this even in the natural order that surrounds us; for example, in the plant kingdom, fertilization occurs through pollen grains that are dispersed by the millions, yet only a few of them flower and cause growth, and it is similar regarding kernels and seeds, etc.

Thank you for writing the details regarding Kfar Chabad in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established, and the development of their settlement.

In accordance with the traditional Jewish custom, as we approach the Festival of Matzos, the time of our freedom, I will conclude with heartfelt wishes for a kosher and joyous holiday and for true freedom in matters of both body and soul alike—true freedom from all the matters that confuse and hinder each person in the fulfillment of the task that Divine Providence has placed upon him.

With respect and blessing.

#### ***7,224***

**Yet only a few of them flower:** See also above, Vol. 14, Letter 5,050, and the references in the notes there.

## 7265

B"H, 13 Nissan, 5721
Brooklyn

To the congregants and gabbaim of the Nusach HaAri Beis Medrash in Montreal,
May Hashem protect them and grant them life,

Peace and blessing!

I received your letter of 29 Shevat in good time, and I was pleased to hear that the words of hisorerus brought to you by the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing man, etc., Rabbi [Name] found a warm resonance; I never had any doubt that they would. For as Chazal say, "I sleep, but my heart is awake"—I am asleep in the galus, but my heart is awake to Torah and mitzvos. I also hope—and this is the main thing—that this hisorerus will be translated into concrete action in all three areas that my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, so frequently emphasized: ahavas Hashem, ahavas HaTorah, and ahavas Yisrael.

As we now stand in the days of Pesach, you are surely aware of what Chazal tell us: that for the Exodus from Mitzrayim, there had to be the full six hundred thousand of Bnei Yisrael, and had even one been missing, it would, G-d forbid, have hindered all the rest. The lesson for us in these words, especially during the days of Pesach, is that we must all remember and exert our greatest efforts to bring every single Yid—man and woman, young and old—closer to Torah and Yiddishkeit. This is essential not only for the one being brought closer, but for all of us.

This is also one of the allusions in the beginning of the Pesach Seder: before we begin the Seder, we proclaim and announce, "Let all who are hungry come and eat; let all who are in need come and celebrate Pesach." This refers both to the literal sense—material assistance—as well as spiritual assistance. Only after this can we say, "Now we are here, next year in the Land of Yisrael; now we are slaves, next year we will be free men."

I wish you all and your families, may they live, a kosher and happy Pesach. I hope to hear good news from you in all matters, and may we very soon merit the fulfillment of the request we voice and pray for: "Next year in the Land of Yisrael, as free men," through Moshiach Tzidkeinu, speedily and in our days.

With blessings for a kosher and happy holiday,

## 726

B"H, Erev Chag HaMatzos, 5721
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the Committee of the Sephardic Community in Jerusalem, Holy City, may it be rebuilt and established,
May Hashem keep them alive,

Peace and Blessing!

Your letter reached me after a long delay, followed by your publications, and I thank you for your thoughtfulness in sending them to me.

Regarding the content of your letter concerning the issues of the Chief Rabbinate—or more accurately, the issue of the Chief Rabbi, the Rishon LeTzion—even though on the face of it this is no longer current, nevertheless, in accordance with the principle of "And you shall be clear before Hashem and before Israel," I will clarify my position once again, even though it has seemingly been publicized sufficiently. It is as follows:

In the present case, this is not a private matter at all, but rather a matter of principle. For this reason, it concerns not only all our brethren, the Children of Israel in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established, but all the Children of Israel in all their places of residence, and it touches upon the honor of the Rabbinate, which is, of course, bound up with the honor of the Torah. The question is: Is it permissible according to our Torah, the Torah of life, to remove a Rabbi from his holy position, especially by means of a committee a significant portion of which consists of people who are entirely secular? Since the position of the Shulchan Aruch on this is simple and clear—in completely rejecting such a matter—the question regarding the election of this or that person as a replacement is resolved automatically.

As to your question: if there is no room for removing a Rabbi from his position by the aforementioned committee, then what is the meaning of limiting a Rabbi’s service to a specific term, followed by elections by such a committee? A difficulty does not, Heaven forbid, nullify a law, especially a law that concerns the honor of the Torah. Performing an act that is contrary to the Shulchan Aruch in the past, and announcing that they will do so in the future, does not transform a transgression into a mitzvah.

With respect and blessing for a kosher and joyous Pesach, and for true liberation from Mitzrayim, from limitations, and from all matters that distract from the service of Hashem with joy and a glad heart.

#### ***726***

**My position:** See also above, letter 714, and the references in the notes there.

## 7228

B"H, 18 Nissan, 5721
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi Naftali, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of the book, *Divrei Shaul*, which you published; thank you, thank you.

Since this comes as a continuation of our conversation when we met, this is surely also an opportunity to mention a primary point we touched upon then: regarding the utilization of one’s talents and knowledge for the dissemination of both the revealed Torah and Chassidus, for it is one Torah.

For if in all times and in all places we were commanded to do so, how much more so in this era of ours, the *ikvesa d’Meshicha*. As is known from the *Iggeres HaKodesh* of the Baal Shem Tov, that when his fountains spread outward, the Master, King Moshiach, will come. And in this country, where we see tangibly that it is becoming a center for our people, the Children of Israel—not only a physical center, but a spiritual one as well, and the wise will understand.

Therefore, the responsibility—and along with it, the privilege—rests with everyone who has the ability to act in this regard, and to act in the style of our Sages, as is proper to do. It is well known that with every commandment of the Torah, the ability is also granted to fulfill that commandment, especially in the aforementioned matter, which is a *zchus harabim*.

May it be the will of Hashem that as we stand in the time of our freedom—freedom from all matters that cause confusion—so too shall it be regarding the aforementioned, and I will be pleased to hear good news in this regard.

With respect and blessing,

Due to the sanctity of the festival, the Rebbe shlita did not sign personally, and I sign on his behalf,

The Secretary
